<div id="review_2317692528" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2317692528" />
    <a title="Roman Clodia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/56111743-roman-clodia"><img alt="Roman Clodia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1464021031p2/56111743.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2317692528?book_show_action=true">Mar 05, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Roman Clodia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Roman Clodia" href="/user/show/56111743-roman-clodia">Roman Clodia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/28100227-beware-of-pity">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2317692528" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12475916658306389286">I'd heard good things about Zweig but gosh, this book is unconvincing melodrama. There's the germ of a taut novella here but dragging the whole thing out to 450 pages wore me down. There's just so much of everything: some relevant, a whole load just waffle. <br/><br/>Although written in 1938, this has the turgid feel of something far older. We might not know, from the book, that Hitler has annexed Austria and that WW2 is close to starting - instead the assassination of Archduke Ferdinand, one of the cause</span>
  <span id="freeText12475916658306389286" style="display:none">I'd heard good things about Zweig but gosh, this book is unconvincing melodrama. There's the germ of a taut novella here but dragging the whole thing out to 450 pages wore me down. There's just so much of everything: some relevant, a whole load just waffle. <br /><br />Although written in 1938, this has the turgid feel of something far older. We might not know, from the book, that Hitler has annexed Austria and that WW2 is close to starting - instead the assassination of Archduke Ferdinand, one of the causes of WW1, is used to interrupt the tiresome 'affair' of the paralysed Edith and her reluctant fiance, Hofmiller. <br /><br />The latter, supposedly a hero of WW1, shows himself to be a fool and a moral coward, passively allowing himself to be dragged into situations merely, it seems, to artificially ramp up the angst. Faced with a difficult occasion what does he do? Flee. Not once, but repeatedly. <br /><br />To fill out the story further, we have various tale-within-a-tale episodes as minor characters step forward to tell their own stories.<br /><br />There's an interesting premise here which might have made a piquant and telling novella - stretched to this overlong length, it loses direction rapidly. It was a relief to finish this.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12475916658306389286" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1849946135" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer9896521880609573556">A tragedy in many senses.<br/>A tragedy for Edith for being considered a cripple and not a person.<br/>A tragedy for the Lieutenant for being so naive and trapped within the rigid world of the Austro-Hungarian army.<br/>A tragedy for Edith's father who so wanted Edith to be well.<br/>A tragedy for Edith's doctor who could see what was happening and could not prevent it.<br/>A tragedy for all for the ending.<br/>A tragedy for everyone living in their cocoon world who did not see a world war coming.<br/>Zweig is able to paint the p</span>
  <span id="freeText9896521880609573556" style="display:none">A tragedy in many senses.<br />A tragedy for Edith for being considered a cripple and not a person.<br />A tragedy for the Lieutenant for being so naive and trapped within the rigid world of the Austro-Hungarian army.<br />A tragedy for Edith's father who so wanted Edith to be well.<br />A tragedy for Edith's doctor who could see what was happening and could not prevent it.<br />A tragedy for all for the ending.<br />A tragedy for everyone living in their cocoon world who did not see a world war coming.<br />Zweig is able to paint the picture a vivid impact of pity to both the pitier and the pitiee, the guilt derived when best intentions go wrong, when honesty is lost through immaturity and regret ends in further regret.<br />Yes, the Lieutenant is weak emotionally, inexperienced with women and unable to see a bigger picture. Yes, the writing contains long passages of anguish, guilt, pity and dithering. But it is so well written, so tragic and so honest any weakness is covered by it's brilliance.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_219491896" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/219491896" />
    <a title="Chuck LoPresti" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2457963-chuck-lopresti"><img alt="Chuck LoPresti" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1308763901p2/2457963.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/219491896?book_show_action=true">Oct 05, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chuck LoPresti" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chuck LoPresti" href="/user/show/2457963-chuck-lopresti">Chuck LoPresti</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer219491896" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13465660517642190225">Beware of Pity is not going to please anybody that isn't willing to tolerate some anachronistic histrionics in the process of telling a pretty engaging story. The plot is fairly simple: an officer can't stand to offend so he allows himself to be manipulated by a family in need of a love-object/hero to save their invalid daughter. That alone would hardly merit 4 stars but Zweig is a great story teller and what is most important here is the psychological insights of a friend of Freud. Stepping ins</span>
  <span id="freeText13465660517642190225" style="display:none">Beware of Pity is not going to please anybody that isn't willing to tolerate some anachronistic histrionics in the process of telling a pretty engaging story. The plot is fairly simple: an officer can't stand to offend so he allows himself to be manipulated by a family in need of a love-object/hero to save their invalid daughter. That alone would hardly merit 4 stars but Zweig is a great story teller and what is most important here is the psychological insights of a friend of Freud. Stepping inside someone's head can be a nauseating experience. Bernhard does a similar thing in Correction - not in the same style - but with the same results. Where the characters in Correction repeat themselves, revealing the sometimes less-directed wanderings of the neurotic mind, Zweig's characters' thoughts take more of a concise but no less revealing tone. Correction and Beware of Pity both made me want to inflict horrible violence on the characters and I'm fairly certain that's not a bad thing - I enjoyed both reads. It's almost like watching a decent horror flick with a huge audience that is all screaming warnings to the hapless kill-fodder - it's silly - but it's fun. Like a drunk to the bar - our officer keeps feeding his horrible addiction to pity until tragedy strikes. I can imagine this was much more fun to read 50 years ago when moral codes and psychological insights were a bit more compelling. It also reminded me of Witkacy's Insatiability a bit in that the shock of the subject matter had greater power to challenge normalicy. Now such histrionics operate more like historical reminders than mental upheavals but that didn't stop me from turning quickly though the pages waiting to see how it all unfolds. A similar type of atmosphere is seen in early silent drama (Von Sternberg for example) where overacting was the norm as a response to the missing narrative. Perhaps it all seems a bit labored now - but it didn't stop me from greatly enjoying this. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13465660517642190225" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer12539619195001250453">"Beware of Pity" ("The Heart's Impatience" in German) is a 1939 novel by the Austrian writer Stefan Zweig. <br/> <br/>A few thoughts: <br/> <br/>It's a brilliant - though melancholy - exploration of compassion and pity. <br/>It's also a fascinating study of human psychology and the interior realms. <br/>It reminded me of "Notes From Underground" by Fyodor Dostoevsky (both have an antihero protagonist and explore human psychology). <br/>I also detect a parallel between the Anton/Edith relationship and Dostoevsky's narrator/prostit</span>
  <span id="freeText12539619195001250453" style="display:none">"Beware of Pity" ("The Heart's Impatience" in German) is a 1939 novel by the Austrian writer Stefan Zweig. <br /> <br />A few thoughts: <br /> <br />It's a brilliant - though melancholy - exploration of compassion and pity. <br />It's also a fascinating study of human psychology and the interior realms. <br />It reminded me of "Notes From Underground" by Fyodor Dostoevsky (both have an antihero protagonist and explore human psychology). <br />I also detect a parallel between the Anton/Edith relationship and Dostoevsky's narrator/prostitute relationship. <br />I loved Doctor Condor - a great character. <br /> <br />Here's a passage: <br /><br />"If so, then Condor was right: If you help only one other human being you have made sense of your life. And it was truly rewarding to sacrifice yourself for others to the full extent of your powers and even beyond. Then any sacrifice was worthwhile. Even a lie that made others happy was more important than the truth." <br /> <br />Zweig explores the claims of the above passage - especially the last one. Can there be love without truth? <br /> <br />Rating: 4 out of 5 stars <br /> <br />Notes: <br />Audiobook: <br /> <br />Narrated by: Nicholas Boulton <br />Length: 14 hours and 43 minutes <br />Unabridged <br />Release date: 2017-06-20 <br />Language: English <br />Publisher: Ukemi Audiobooks </span>

<div id="review_2109445705" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2109445705" />
    <a title="Chrissie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie"><img alt="Chrissie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1321799585p2/632247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2109445705?book_show_action=true">Oct 29, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chrissie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chrissie" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie">Chrissie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=austria">austria</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=audible-uk">audible-uk</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=hf">hf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=medical">medical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=2017-read">2017-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=hungary">hungary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=philo-psychol">philo-psychol</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=love">love</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2109445705" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14352807825343628801">Reading books written by authors from different nations gives you a feel for respective countries and cultures. I enjoy this. Zweig captures life in the Austro-Hungarian Empire before its fall. Knowledge of times past helps us better understand modern cultures and traditions; the past puts a stamp on the future and therefore I like reading about the past. Anyhow, the book is a story within a story. The person who supposedly has written the story is relating what he was told by a friend in 1938, </span>
  <span id="freeText14352807825343628801" style="display:none">Reading books written by authors from different nations gives you a feel for respective countries and cultures. I enjoy this. Zweig captures life in the Austro-Hungarian Empire before its fall. Knowledge of times past helps us better understand modern cultures and traditions; the past puts a stamp on the future and therefore I like reading about the past. Anyhow, the book is a story within a story. The person who supposedly has written the story is relating what he was told by a friend in 1938, so on the brink of the Second World War. His friend's story, which constitutes the story of the book, is set in a garrison town on the Hungarian Austrian border preceding the outbreak of the First World War. Neither war is the focus of the book, but life in the military is, as well as life of aristocrats, or to be more accurate "so-called" aristocrats. The manners and the morals of each. <br /><br />It is interesting to compare the writing styles of authors from different countries. French literature does not read as English or American or Scandinavian or Spanish or German or........ literature. Do you follow my point? This is another reason to read this book. The writing is articulate and clear and precise, and German in style. <br /><br />The central theme is pity. Look what is written here in the book:<br /><br />"There are two kinds of pity. One, the weak and sentimental kind, which is really no more than the heart's impatience to be rid as quickly as possible of the painful emotion aroused by the sight of another's unhappiness, that pity which is not compassion, but only an instinctive desire to fortify one's own soul against the sufferings of another; and the other, the only one that counts, the unsentimental but creative kind, which knows what it is about and is determined to hold out, in patience and forbearance, to the very limit of its strength and even beyond."<br /><br />There is sentimental pity; this is self-oriented. Then there is pity based on compassion; this is very different. One demands nothing of a person; the other demands strength. What are the consequences of actions based on pity, be it one or the other? In the book one character displays one kind of pity and another the other. These two central characters, a twenty-five-year-old cavalry officer named Lieutenant Anton Hoffmiller contrasts with forty-seven-year-old Doctor Condor. The supporting characters' pity may be studied too. And how does the person who is pitied feel, respond and behave? We are given seventeen-year-old Edith von Kekesfalva, once healthy, vivacious and happy, now lame, temperamental and spoiled by everyone around her. Watching the consequences of actions gives the reader a jolt. Sometimes one may not think ahead; doing something “kind” may cause more problems than one may imagine. <br /><br />What gave me trouble with the book? First of all, I know I could never behave as <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Anton <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> does. I know I would have to lay everything above board <b>and</b> his wavering, his inability to make up his mind drags the action out and makes the book too long. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Anton does not love Edith, and he never has loved her. He only loves the glory of acting the part of a “knight in shining armor”. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> And actually I would be fine with this if the author created empathy for him in me; I felt no empathy for <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Anton. Who of us hasn´t had the guts to break off a relationship because they know it has no future?! Most of us have the courage to do this! <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> Thirdly, there are two long stories plunked into the central tale. We are told the history of both the doctor and a military officer. They read as two separate novellas. We are <u>
  <b>told</b>
</u> their stories. The reader does not live their experiences. I think the information should have been woven in better, in some other manner.<br /><br />I have given the audiobook narration by Nicholas Boulton five stars. Good speed, easy to follow and correct pronunciation of foreign words. <br /><br />I am glad I read the book. I probably will read more by the author, but this is his only full-length novel and it is for this reason I chose it. His other works are short stories or novellas and some biographies. Now I would like to read a biography about him. He and his second wife, Lotte Altman, committed suicide together in 1942, in Brazil, in despair at the state of the world.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14352807825343628801" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2196793781" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2196793781" />
    <a title="Brendan Monroe" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22409011-brendan-monroe"><img alt="Brendan Monroe" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1565543383p2/22409011.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2196793781?book_show_action=true">Nov 27, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brendan Monroe" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brendan Monroe" href="/user/show/22409011-brendan-monroe">Brendan Monroe</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141196411" href="/book/show/25242048-impatience-of-the-heart">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22409011-brendan-monroe?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22409011-brendan-monroe?shelf=international">international</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22409011-brendan-monroe?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22409011-brendan-monroe?shelf=loved">loved</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22409011-brendan-monroe?shelf=austrian">austrian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22409011-brendan-monroe?shelf=essential">essential</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22409011-brendan-monroe?shelf=world-war-i">world-war-i</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22409011-brendan-monroe?shelf=great-character-alert">great-character-alert</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2196793781" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9370802286361435388">Every so often you’ll read a book that just floors you with its greatness. If we’re lucky we might read one or two of these books a year, but the number is often much less than that. There just aren’t many of these kinds of books getting published anymore, so to find a really great one it’s often necessary to reach back and pull one off the mighty heap that history has given us.<br/><br/>Stefan Zweig’s “Impatience of the Heart” is one of those books. Writing about it now leaves me fearful of tripping all </span>
  <span id="freeText9370802286361435388" style="display:none">Every so often you’ll read a book that just floors you with its greatness. If we’re lucky we might read one or two of these books a year, but the number is often much less than that. There just aren’t many of these kinds of books getting published anymore, so to find a really great one it’s often necessary to reach back and pull one off the mighty heap that history has given us.<br /><br />Stefan Zweig’s “Impatience of the Heart” is one of those books. Writing about it now leaves me fearful of tripping all over myself in an attempt to accurately describe it and its impact on me. Yes, this is one of those books that leaves a mark. <br /><br />There are those who have read “Impatience of the Heart” and there is everyone else. Similarly, you will learn something about yourself while reading it and something about everyone else. It’s one of the most essential books I’ve ever read for better understanding human beings and their motivations.<br /><br />I am fearful, perhaps, because I am aware of the importance of the review. Even a review like this - which won’t be published in any national syndication, but that I write mainly as an encapsulation of my present feelings to later return to – I struggle to write because of its potential to convince even one person of whether or not to give this book a shot.<br /><br />In reality, all reviews are little more than an appeal. The majority seem designed to appeal to those who have no familiarity with the thing under review. They’re designed to first answer the question, “Would reading/watching/listening to X be a good use of my time?” <br /><br />We are all drawn to reviewers who we believe like/dislike the same things we do. But a good reviewer is also adept at being able to craft a convincing appeal. <br /><br />Time is precious, and books require more of it than any other single art form I know. It makes sense then that we should be selective with the books we choose, conducting research beforehand on whether reading any given book is likely to yield at least the minimum of what we want to get out of it. We sometimes waive this requirement for those books considered “classics” but even then there are far too many supposed classics out there to be able to devote time to reading them all.<br /><br />That’s why a book is often an intensely personal experience. We tend to read on our own and then, unless what we’re reading is the latest Dan Brown novel, we’re often in a community where we know maybe one or two people, at best, who’ve read the same book. And, of course, experiences differ, which brings us to the second type of review.<br /><br />This type of review is written as an appeal to those who are already familiar with the thing under review. The critic here is trying to foster agreement and/or provide insight into the thing being reviewed. <br /><br />After reading a book and then writing my own review of it, I read the reviews other people have left. I’ve read many reviews over time that have shaped my opinion of a book and that have served to enrich it. I never do this before writing my own review, however, because I want my most recent impression of the book to be formed out of the author’s own words, not the thoughts of a critic. <br /><br />I look forward to reading other people’s thoughts. But, with this book more than most, those thoughts have the potential to more greatly affect me. I really don’t know how I’ll handle someone’s negative review of this book, though I am interested in understanding how their overall impression was negative. <br /><br />“Impatience of the Heart” is one of the greatest books I’ve ever read. Zweig’s writing is enchantingly plain, but it’s ornamented with nostalgic gems that conjure up lost empires, in this case, that of Austria-Hungary. It’s as good as a book can be, and its greatness is uniquely literary. “Impatience of the Heart” couldn’t be adapted for the screen or brought to any other format without losing something essential in the process. <br /><br />It is very, very good at capturing what it is in us that makes us so weak and powerless. It is also a cautionary tale about the dangers of that “weak and sentimental” pity that Zweig describes in the following passage:<br /><br />“There are two kinds of pity. One, the weak and sentimental kind, which is really no more than the heart's impatience to be rid as quickly as possible of the painful emotion aroused by the sight of another's unhappiness, that pity which is not compassion, but only an instinctive desire to fortify one's own soul against the sufferings of another; and the other, the only one that counts, the unsentimental but creative kind, which knows what it is about and is determined to hold out, in patience and forbearance, to the very limit of its strength and even beyond.”<br /><br />The purpose of reviews is to get books like “Impatience of the Heart” read and savored as they deserve. Unmissable.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9370802286361435388" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1074431359" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1074431359" />
    <a title="Nigeyb" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10301421-nigeyb"><img alt="Nigeyb" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593065725p2/10301421.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1074431359?book_show_action=true">Oct 07, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nigeyb" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nigeyb" href="/user/show/10301421-nigeyb">Nigeyb</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13790841926099825571">A very powerful work and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/25573.Stefan_Zweig' title='Stefan Zweig' rel='nofollow noopener'>Stefan Zweig</a>'s only full length novel. <br/><br/><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/25573.Stefan_Zweig' title='Stefan Zweig' rel='nofollow noopener'>Stefan Zweig</a> generally cut and cut his longer stories until arriving at the essence of the tale. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17434867.Beware_of_Pity' title='Beware of Pity by Stefan Zweig' rel='nofollow noopener'>Beware of Pity</a> is therefore an anomaly, one that forces me to conclude he should have written more novels.<br/><br/>Memorable characters abound in this book that actually contains three extraordinary stories, the primary one set against the lead up to World War One.   <span id="freeText13790841926099825571" style="display:none">A very powerful work and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/25573.Stefan_Zweig" title="Stefan Zweig" rel="nofollow noopener">Stefan Zweig</a>'s only full length novel. <br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/25573.Stefan_Zweig" title="Stefan Zweig" rel="nofollow noopener">Stefan Zweig</a> generally cut and cut his longer stories until arriving at the essence of the tale. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17434867.Beware_of_Pity" title="Beware of Pity by Stefan Zweig" rel="nofollow noopener">Beware of Pity</a> is therefore an anomaly, one that forces me to conclude he should have written more novels.<br /><br />Memorable characters abound in this book that actually contains three extraordinary stories, the primary one set against the lead up to World War One. The protagonist, Lieutenant Anton Hofmiller is an idealistic Austrian army officer and it is his pity, something of a double edged sword, which is at the root of this tragedy. <br /><br />Had <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/25573.Stefan_Zweig" title="Stefan Zweig" rel="nofollow noopener">Stefan Zweig</a> written more novels I would have already added them to my "to read" list, as it is at least he created this one memorable work. It is well worth reading.<br /><br />I'll also take this opportunity to recommend the only other book I have read by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/25573.Stefan_Zweig" title="Stefan Zweig" rel="nofollow noopener">Stefan Zweig</a>, and that is his fascinating memoir <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/629429.The_World_of_Yesterday" title="The World of Yesterday by Stefan Zweig" rel="nofollow noopener">The World of Yesterday</a>. <br /><br />4/5</span>

<div id="review_180271558" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Sharyl" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2475858-sharyl"><img alt="Sharyl" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1489234521p2/2475858.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/180271558?book_show_action=true">Jun 28, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sharyl" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sharyl" href="/user/show/2475858-sharyl">Sharyl</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780810113305" href="/book/show/1240899.Beware_of_Pity">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2475858-sharyl?shelf=2016-reads">2016-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer180271558" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7939627562147068444">Here is a novel that would make for an interesting discussion...how much responsibility does anyone have for someone else's emotions? At what point does a situation become emotional blackmail? Who is being abused? How far would you compromise your life's path for the hope of making someone else happier?<br/><br/>Anthony Hoffmiller, a sad war hero, tells the tale of what transpired in his youth, something that led to the soul killing guilt that enabled him to not fear death during battle, that will make hi</span>
  <span id="freeText7939627562147068444" style="display:none">Here is a novel that would make for an interesting discussion...how much responsibility does anyone have for someone else's emotions? At what point does a situation become emotional blackmail? Who is being abused? How far would you compromise your life's path for the hope of making someone else happier?<br /><br />Anthony Hoffmiller, a sad war hero, tells the tale of what transpired in his youth, something that led to the soul killing guilt that enabled him to not fear death during battle, that will make him feel shame until the day he dies. <br /><br />I'll admit, toward the end of this novel, some of Lt. Hoffmiller's angst began to seem tedious to me, but then--perhaps that was the intended effect. This emotional turmoil turns a young officer into an emotional, exhausted wreck--and I felt it. <br /><br />Just as Hoffmiller's story is coming to a climax, Archduke Franz Ferdinand is assassinated, which precipitates the beginning of WWI, and that makes this young man's woes seem insignificant, and yet--<br /><br />There are no winners here. That's not a spoiler. <br /><br />I can see why this is a classic...it's a very thought-provoking, well-written novel which asks timeless questions.<br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7939627562147068444" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_84254731" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/84254731" />
    <a title="Jan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1492258-jan"><img alt="Jan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1244689635p2/1492258.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/84254731?book_show_action=true">Jan 10, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jan" href="/user/show/1492258-jan">Jan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer84254731" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18100033798287247536">"Beware of Pity" is a great psychological portrait of an officer in the Austrian army, right before the outbreak of the great war. At the outset, young Anton Hofmiller commits a terrible social gaffe by asking the crippled daughter of a local noble to dance, and it is in saving face that he embarks on a journey of pity, honor, and obligation, plunging ever deeper into her life and her family's.<br/><br/>Stefan Zweig paints an excellent portrait of a young man torn by conflicting feelings, constantly turni</span>
  <span id="freeText18100033798287247536" style="display:none">"Beware of Pity" is a great psychological portrait of an officer in the Austrian army, right before the outbreak of the great war. At the outset, young Anton Hofmiller commits a terrible social gaffe by asking the crippled daughter of a local noble to dance, and it is in saving face that he embarks on a journey of pity, honor, and obligation, plunging ever deeper into her life and her family's.<br /><br />Stefan Zweig paints an excellent portrait of a young man torn by conflicting feelings, constantly turning over in his head what he is to do but yet faltering when it comes time to make each critical decision. Anton is simply too weak to resist the warm glow of approval and acceptance that comes with being a knight in shining armor for young Edith Kekesfalva, and even though he is repulsed by her, he engages in emotional deceit because he would rather continue to play his self-appointed role of manliness and chivalry than to be honest about his feelings. <br /><br />In honest moments, pity is reveled in as a feeling of power; at others, it is masked as an honorable consecration to another's happiness, and the author does a fantastic job of making this two-sided sword both identifiable and vile. The feelings that are portrayed in this novel are palpably real, and every reader can identify with the sort of moral weakness where we have built for ourselves an attitude and a posture into which doing the right thing will not fit: an emotional play-acting where honesty does not enter into the role. It is almost viscerally painful to see Zweig develop this to the most extreme conclusion.<br /><br />What is most fascinating about this story is the undercurrent of impending war, and the knowledge that it's being told after twenty years. The narrator himself downplays his subsequent bravery in the war and implies that it is nothing compared to the moral courage that he ought to have displayed in the summer of 1914, and yet did not.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18100033798287247536" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Roberto" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Roberto" href="/user/show/5870798-roberto">Roberto</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2051017244" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12715810772818411661">A great premise, though i'm not fully sure what Zweig was getting at about pity, especially during the context of the second world war when this was written. But this was a great, nerve-wracking, melodramatic, (it says 'feverish' on the jacket and that it is) read which starts off with a small, could-happen-to-anyone kinda incident and just builds and builds, slowly, logically until you are hurtling downhill fast, and there were bits which were agonising to read ughhh. <br/><br/>It is a masterclass of nar</span>
  <span id="freeText12715810772818411661" style="display:none">A great premise, though i'm not fully sure what Zweig was getting at about pity, especially during the context of the second world war when this was written. But this was a great, nerve-wracking, melodramatic, (it says 'feverish' on the jacket and that it is) read which starts off with a small, could-happen-to-anyone kinda incident and just builds and builds, slowly, logically until you are hurtling downhill fast, and there were bits which were agonising to read ughhh. <br /><br />It is a masterclass of narrative, a real pageturner. I will say that this book is problematic is some ways, there is a bunch of ableism in the text...from the use of disability as plot device, as well as the derogatory language and 'otherness' laid on the character of Edith, and yeah i know a lot of this is character-based ignorance or naivety on the part of the narrator and his fellow officers etc, as well as being indicative of the times, but it's there, and it's weird. But i really enjoyed this book, and never found it boring once, despite some quite drawn out segments and it being way long for Bert.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12715810772818411661" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_492626419" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/492626419" />
    <a title="Sarah Rogers" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4681572-sarah-rogers"><img alt="Sarah Rogers" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1566351724p2/4681572.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/492626419?book_show_action=true">Jan 02, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sarah Rogers" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sarah Rogers" href="/user/show/4681572-sarah-rogers">Sarah Rogers</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer492626419" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4250236112004647567">The doorman in my building (a Romanian named Moses) recommended this book to me. He told me "it is life." <br/><br/>After I read it, I understand what he meant. Everything in this book seems so familiar, even though it takes place in the early twentieth century, and revolves around an Austrian cavalry officer's relationship with a high society family. It fires off insight after insight into human psychology with every new revelation. <br/><br/>It's also beautifully written. You know how you tend to skim over sectio</span>
  <span id="freeText4250236112004647567" style="display:none">The doorman in my building (a Romanian named Moses) recommended this book to me. He told me "it is life." <br /><br />After I read it, I understand what he meant. Everything in this book seems so familiar, even though it takes place in the early twentieth century, and revolves around an Austrian cavalry officer's relationship with a high society family. It fires off insight after insight into human psychology with every new revelation. <br /><br />It's also beautifully written. You know how you tend to skim over sections in most books? I never skimmed over a page of this novel. The prose is so clean, and the story so engaging, that every sentence was a pleasure to read. I was so engrossed in it that a proprietor of a restaurant where I was eating asked me about the book I was reading: "It must be good!" he said.<br /><br />It was good, yo.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4250236112004647567" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2104494799" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2104494799" />
    <a title="Tara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/70459127-tara"><img alt="Tara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1592666421p2/70459127.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2104494799?book_show_action=true">Aug 26, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tara" href="/user/show/70459127-tara">Tara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/26055488-beware-of-pity">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2104494799" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9256439474628107661"><blockquote>
  <i>“…I began to realize that it is not evil and brutality, but nearly always weakness, that is to blame for the worst things that happen in this world.”</i>
</blockquote><br/>4.5 stars. <br/><br/><i>Beware of Pity</i> was a very thorough analysis of the often insidious nature of pity. The book explored the downward spiral of mutual wretchedness that can arise between the one who pities and the one pitied. The examination of the struggle that ensued when Hofmiller tried to force himself to love the creature he merely pitied, and the r</span>
  <span id="freeText9256439474628107661" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>“…I began to realize that it is not evil and brutality, but nearly always weakness, that is to blame for the worst things that happen in this world.”</i>
</blockquote><br />4.5 stars. <br /><br /><i>Beware of Pity</i> was a very thorough analysis of the often insidious nature of pity. The book explored the downward spiral of mutual wretchedness that can arise between the one who pities and the one pitied. The examination of the struggle that ensued when Hofmiller tried to force himself to love the creature he merely pitied, and the resulting repugnance and desperate desire to flee: absolutely brilliant. Zweig’s psychological insight was truly remarkable.<br /><blockquote>
  <i>“But this apparently helpless old man is a djinn, an evil spirit, a scoundrelly magician, and no sooner is he seated on the young man’s shoulders than he clamps his hairy, naked thighs round his benefactor’s throat in a vice-like grip and cannot be dislodged. Mercilessly he makes of the young man who has taken pity on him a beast of burden, spurs him on and on, pitilessly, relentlessly, never granting him a moment’s rest. The luckless young man is obliged to carry him wherever he asks, and from now on has no will of his own. He has become the beast of burden, the slave, of the old rascal: no matter if his knees give and his lips are parched with thirst, he is compelled, foolish victim of his own pity, to trot on and on, is fated to drag the wicked, infamous, cunning old man along for ever on his back.”</i>
</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="9256439474628107661" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('vI1qC/weUT2fFK/+livelECUulOhbOCCWDXKo2STRHzEQjr5jLpNEyq6rMdns/MTZ+oIULC/38vSstFM32C7Kw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('otbARg6W034sp3f0ZFEM8s4xwLWHeYMJhIOydZMtOu3aGZC0fjLPUJkJdM2VySF16U9ytpaqvEAOBKmaKN7Fug==')})
  }

Shooting parties in great country houses, turbulent scene<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText14084259771211680061\" style=\"display:none\">Painting an unrivalled portrait of the vanished world of pre-1914 Hungary, this story is told through the eyes of two young Transylvanian cousins, Count Balint Abady and Count Laszlo Gyeroffy. Shooting parties in great country houses, turbulent scenes in parliament, and the luxury of life in Budapest provide the backdrop for this gripping, prescient novel, forming a chilling indictment of upper-class frivolity and political folly, in which good manners cloak indifference and brutality. Abady becomes aware of the plight of a group of Romanian mountain peasants and champions their cause, while Gyeroffy dissipates his resources at the gaming tables, mirroring the decline of the Austro-Hungarian Empire itself. Į klestinčią kunigaikščio Boriatinskio sodybą, apsuptą lūžtančio nuo gėrybių sodo. Ir sodyba, ir pats kunigaikščių gyvenimas – kaip šventė: amžina, dosni, džiūgaujanti. Tačiau į šį gyvenimą ateina vėlyvas ir niekieno nelaukiamas vaikas. Dukra, kurios gimimas sugriauna idealiai atrodžiusią šeimą. Tusia nuo gimimo yra kitokia. Patriarchalinėje, tradicijų sukaustytoje visuomenėje, kurioje kiekvienas elgiasi ir gyvena taip, kaip diktuoja luomo taisyklės, ji atrodo, lyg būtų atėjusi iš kito laiko. Ji nuo gimimo daro ką nori, elgiasi kaip neįprastai laisvai, šokiruodama aplinkinius. Ji pati sprendžia, kada jai pradėti kalbėti. Kaip elgtis, kuo domėtis, ką mylėti ir ko neapkęsti – o tai neatleistina toje aplinkoje, kurioje jai tenka gyventi.\n„Sodas“ – knyga, skirta pasiilgusiems klasikinės rusų literatūros. Tai epinis, plačiais mostais nutapytas anuometinės Rusijos paveikslas. Didžiulis Boriatinskių dvaras – tarsi rusiškas pasaulio modelis: su pirkliais ir valstiečiais, prietarais ir pažanga, mergomis patarnautojomis, sėja ir derliaus nuėmimu, sodu, kiaulėmis ir arkliais. Ypač arkliais, kurie Tusiai reiškia daugiau nei žmonės. Visą šį universumą valdo dviese: Nadežda Aleksandrovna Boriatinskaja ir Tusiai ateiti į pasaulį padėjęs vokiečių kilmės daktaras Meizelis. His novels, short stories, and biographies were so compelling that they became instant best sellers. Zweig was also an intellectual and a lover of all the arts, high and low. Yet after Hitler’s rise to power, this celebrated writer who had dedicated so much energy to promoting international humanism plummeted, in a matter of a few years, into an increasingly isolated exile—from London to Bath to New York City, then Ossining, Rio, and finally Petrópolis—where, in 1942, in a cramped bungalow, he killed himself.  The Impossible Exile tells the tragic story of Zweig’s extraordinary rise and fall while it also depicts, with great acumen, the gulf between the world of ideas in Europe and in America, and the consuming struggle of those forced to forsake one for the other. It also reveals how Zweig embodied, through his work, thoughts, and behavior, the end of an era—the implosion of Europe as an ideal of Western civilization.<\/span>\n  <a       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/25573.Stefan_Zweig">About Stefan Zweig</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/25573.Stefan_Zweig"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1190140738p3/25573.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/25573.Stefan_Zweig">Stefan Zweig
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
5,937
followers
</div>

</div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer11085392803099834363">Stefan Zweig was one of the world's most famous writers during the 1920s and 1930s, especially in the U.S., South America, and Europe. He produced novels, plays, biographies, and journalist pieces. Among his most famous works are <i>Beware of Pity</i>, <i>Letter from an Unknown Woman</i>, and <i>Mary, Queen of Scotland and the Isles</i>. He and his second wife committed suicide in 1942.<br/><br/>Zweig studied in Austria, France</span>
  <span id="freeText11085392803099834363" style="display:none">Stefan Zweig was one of the world's most famous writers during the 1920s and 1930s, especially in the U.S., South America, and Europe. He produced novels, plays, biographies, and journalist pieces. Among his most famous works are <i>Beware of Pity</i>, <i>Letter from an Unknown Woman</i>, and <i>Mary, Queen of Scotland and the Isles</i>. He and his second wife committed suicide in 1942.<br /><br />Zweig studied in Austria, France, and Germany before settling in Salzburg in 1913. In 1934, driven into exile by the Nazis, he emigrated to England and then, in 1940, to Brazil by way of New York. Finding only growing loneliness and disillusionment in their new surroundings, he and his second wife committed suicide.<br /><br />Zweig's interest in psychology and the teachings of Sigmund Freud led to his most characteristic work, the subtle portrayal of character. Zweig's essays include studies of Honoré de Balzac, Charles Dickens, and Fyodor Dostoyevsky (<i>Drei Meister</i>, 1920; <i>Three Masters</i>) and of Friedrich Hölderlin, Heinrich von Kleist, and Friedrich Nietzsche (<i>Der Kampf mit dem Dämon</i>, 1925; <i>Master Builders</i>). He achieved popularity with <i>Sternstunden der Menschheit</i> (1928; <i>The Tide of Fortune</i>), five historical portraits in miniature. He wrote full-scale, intuitive rather than objective, biographies of the French statesman Joseph Fouché (1929), Mary Stuart (1935), and others. His stories include those in <i>Verwirrung der Gefühle</i> (1925; <i>Conflicts</i>). He also wrote a psychological novel, <i>Ungeduld des Herzens</i> (1938; <i>Beware of Pity</i>), and translated works of Charles Baudelaire, Paul Verlaine, and Emile Verhaeren.<br /><br />Most recently, his works provided the inspiration for 2014 film <i>The Grand Budapest Hotel</i>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11085392803099834363" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/1229574-ungeduld-des-herzens">Quotes from Beware of Pity</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;No guilt is forgotten so long as the conscience still knows of it.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/406443-no-guilt-is-forgotten-so-long-as-the-conscience-still">157 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;There are two kinds of pity. One, the weak and sentimental kind, which is really no more than the heart's impatience to be rid as quickly as possible of the painful emotion aroused by the sight of another's unhappiness, that pity which is not compassion, but only an instinctive desire to fortify one's own soul agains the sufferings of another; and the other, the only one at counts, the unsentimental but creative kind, which knows what it is about and is determined to hold out, in patience and forbearance, to the very limit of its strength and even beyond.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/842623-there-are-two-kinds-of-pity-one-the-weak-and">72 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
